<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Pregnancy</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Time of offspring development in mother's body</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about pregnancy in humans. For other mammals, see <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_(mammals)" title="Pregnancy (mammals)">Pregnancy (mammals)</a>. For fish, see <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_in_fish" title="Pregnancy in fish">Pregnancy in fish</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Pregnancy</b> is the time during which one or more <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a> develops (<a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestates</a>) inside a <a href="/wiki/Woman" title="Woman">woman's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Womb" title="Womb">womb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosby_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosby-13">[13]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Multiple_birth" title="Multiple birth">multiple pregnancy</a> involves more than one offspring, such as with <a href="/wiki/Twin" title="Twin">twins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Pregnancy usually occurs by <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a>, but can also occur through <a href="/wiki/Assisted_reproductive_technology" title="Assisted reproductive technology">assisted reproductive technology</a> procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-She2016_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-She2016-6">[6]</a></sup> A pregnancy may end in a <a href="/wiki/Live_birth_(human)" title="Live birth (human)">live birth</a>, a spontaneous <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> (spontaneous abortion), an <a href="/wiki/Abortion#Induced" title="Abortion">induced abortion</a> (induced miscarriage), or a <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillbirth</a>. <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">Childbirth</a> typically occurs around 40 weeks from the start of the <a href="/wiki/Menstruation#Onset_and_frequency" title="Menstruation">last menstrual period</a> (LMP), a span known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestational_age_(obstetrics)" title="Gestational age (obstetrics)">gestational age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ab2011_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ab2011-5">[5]</a></sup> This is just over nine months. Counting by <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization#Fertilization_age" title="Human fertilization">fertilization age</a>, the length is about 38 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ab2011_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ab2011-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosby_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mosby-13">[13]</a></sup> Pregnancy is "the presence of an implanted human embryo or fetus in the uterus"; <a href="/wiki/Implantation_(embryology)" title="Implantation (embryology)">implantation</a> occurs on average 8–9 days after fertilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> An <i><a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a></i> is the term for the developing offspring during the first seven weeks following implantation (i.e. ten weeks' gestational age), after which the term <i><a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a></i> is used until birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ab2011_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ab2011-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms_of_pregnancy" title="Signs and symptoms of pregnancy">Signs and symptoms of early pregnancy</a> may include <a href="/wiki/Amenorrhea" title="Amenorrhea">missed periods</a>, tender breasts, <a href="/wiki/Morning_sickness" title="Morning sickness">morning sickness</a> (nausea and vomiting), hunger, and frequent urination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Sym_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Sym-1">[1]</a></sup> Pregnancy may be confirmed with a <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_test" title="Pregnancy test">pregnancy test</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2012Diag_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012Diag-7">[7]</a></sup> Methods of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>—or, more accurately, <i>contraception</i>—are used to avoid pregnancy.
</p><p>Pregnancy is divided into three trimesters of approximately three months each. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_trimester" title="First trimester">first trimester</a> includes conception, which is when the sperm fertilizes the egg. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilized_egg" title="Fertilized egg">fertilized egg</a> then travels down the <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube" title="Fallopian tube">Fallopian tube</a> and attaches to the inside of the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>, where it begins to form the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>. During the first trimester, the possibility of miscarriage (natural death of embryo or fetus) is at its highest. Around the middle of the second trimester, movement of the fetus may be felt. At 28 weeks, more than 90% of babies can <a href="/wiki/Fetal_viability" title="Fetal viability">survive outside of the uterus</a> if <a href="/wiki/Neonatal_intensive_care_unit" title="Neonatal intensive care unit">provided with high-quality medical care</a>, though babies born at this time will likely experience serious health complications such as heart and respiratory problems and long-term intellectual and developmental disabilities.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Prenatal_care" title="Prenatal care">Prenatal care</a> improves pregnancy outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Prenatal_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Prenatal-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">Nutrition</a> during pregnancy is important to ensure healthy growth of the fetus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-16">[16]</a></sup>  Prenatal care may also include avoiding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smoking_and_pregnancy" title="Smoking and pregnancy">tobacco smoking</a>, and alcohol, taking regular exercise, having <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood tests</a>, and regular <a href="/wiki/Physical_examination" title="Physical examination">physical examinations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Prenatal_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Prenatal-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_pregnancy" title="Complications of pregnancy">Complications of pregnancy</a> may include <a href="/wiki/Hypertensive_disease_of_pregnancy" title="Hypertensive disease of pregnancy">disorders of high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gestational_diabetes" title="Gestational diabetes">gestational diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron-deficiency_anemia" title="Iron-deficiency anemia">iron-deficiency anemia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hyperemesis_gravidarum" title="Hyperemesis gravidarum">severe nausea and vomiting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Compli_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Compli-3">[3]</a></sup> In the ideal childbirth labor begins on its own when a woman is "at term".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACOGfive-2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACOGfive-2-17">[17]</a></sup> Babies born before 37 weeks are "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preterm" title="Preterm">preterm</a>" and at higher risk of health problems such as <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_palsy" title="Cerebral palsy">cerebral palsy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> Babies born between weeks 37 and 39 are considered "early term" while those born between weeks 39 and 41 are considered "full term".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> Babies born between weeks 41 and 42 weeks are considered "late term" while after 42 weeks they are considered "<a href="/wiki/Postterm_pregnancy" title="Postterm pregnancy">post term</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">Delivery</a> before 39 weeks by <a href="/wiki/Labor_induction" title="Labor induction">labor induction</a> or <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean section</a> is not recommended unless required for other medical reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 213 million pregnancies occurred in 2012, of which, 190 million (89%) were in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a> and 23 million (11%) were in the developed world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sed2014_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sed2014-11">[11]</a></sup> The number of pregnancies in women aged between 15 and 44 is 133 per 1,000 women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sed2014_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sed2014-11">[11]</a></sup> About 10% to 15% of recognized pregnancies end in <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2012_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-John2012-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2016, complications of pregnancy resulted in 230,600 <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">maternal deaths</a>, down from 377,000 deaths in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2016_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2016-12">[12]</a></sup> Common causes include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_bleeding" title="Maternal bleeding">bleeding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Postpartum_infections" title="Postpartum infections">infections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertensive_disease_of_pregnancy" title="Hypertensive disease of pregnancy">hypertensive diseases of pregnancy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obstructed_labor" title="Obstructed labor">obstructed labor</a>, miscarriage, abortion, or <a href="/wiki/Ectopic_pregnancy" title="Ectopic pregnancy">ectopic pregnancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2016_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2016-12">[12]</a></sup> Globally, 44% of pregnancies are <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancy" title="Unintended pregnancy">unplanned</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bea2018_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bea2018-19">[19]</a></sup> Over half (56%) of unplanned pregnancies are aborted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bea2018_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bea2018-19">[19]</a></sup> Among unintended pregnancies in the United States, 60% of the women used <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> to some extent during the month pregnancy began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif"><img alt="Title page from an 18th-century book about pregnancy" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="10274" data-file-width="7364" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anatomia_uteri_humani_gravidi_V00001_00000002.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Hunter_(anatomist)" title="William Hunter (anatomist)">William Hunter</a>, <i>Anatomia uteri humani gravidi tabulis illustrata</i>, 1774</div></div></div>
<p>Associated terms for pregnancy are <i>gravid</i> and <i>parous</i>. <i>Gravidus</i> and <i>gravid</i> come from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word meaning "heavy" and a pregnant female is sometimes referred to as a <i>gravida</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFD_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MFD-21">[21]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Gravidity_and_parity" title="Gravidity and parity">Gravidity</a></i> refers to the number of times that a female has been pregnant. Similarly, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Gravidity_and_parity#Parity" title="Gravidity and parity">parity</a></i> is used for the number of times that a female carries a pregnancy to a <a href="/wiki/Fetal_viability" title="Fetal viability">viable stage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patient_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patient-22">[22]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twins" title="Twins">Twins</a> and other multiple births are counted as one pregnancy and birth. A woman who has never been pregnant is referred to as a <i>nulligravida.</i> A woman who is (or has been only) pregnant for the first time is referred to as a <i>primigravida</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMHP_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-TMHP-23">[23]</a></sup> and a woman in subsequent pregnancies as a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multigravida" title="Multigravida">multigravida</a></i> or as <i>multiparous.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFD_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-MFD-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Therefore, during a second pregnancy a woman would be described as <i>gravida 2, para 1</i> and upon live delivery as <i>gravida 2, para 2.</i> In-progress pregnancies, <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriages</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillbirths</a> account for parity values being less than the gravida number. In the case of a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_birth" title="Multiple birth">multiple birth</a> the gravida number and parity value are increased by one only. Women who have never carried a pregnancy more than 20 weeks are referred to as <i>nulliparous</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>A pregnancy is considered <i>term</i> at 37 weeks of gestation. It is <i>preterm</i> if less than 37 weeks and <i>postterm</i> at or beyond 42 weeks of gestation. American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists have recommended further division with <i>early term</i> 37 weeks up to 39 weeks, <i>full term</i> 39 weeks up to 41 weeks, and <i>late term</i> 41 weeks up to 42 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The terms <i>preterm</i> and <i>postterm</i> have largely replaced earlier terms of <i>premature</i> and <i>postmature</i>. <i>Preterm</i> and <i>postterm</i> are defined above, whereas <i>premature</i> and <i>postmature</i> have historical meaning and relate more to the infant's size and state of development rather than to the stage of pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms_of_pregnancy" title="Signs and symptoms of pregnancy">Signs and symptoms of pregnancy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_pregnancy" title="Complications of pregnancy">Complications of pregnancy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melasmablemish.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Melasmablemish.jpg/220px-Melasmablemish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Melasmablemish.jpg/330px-Melasmablemish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Melasmablemish.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melasmablemish.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Melasma" title="Melasma">Melasma</a>: pigment changes to the face due to pregnancy</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="529" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg/220px-PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg/330px-PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg/440px-PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PregnancyinCrossSection.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the later part of pregnancy the uterus takes up much of the abdomen.</div></div></div>
<p>The usual <a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms_of_pregnancy" title="Signs and symptoms of pregnancy">signs and symptoms of pregnancy</a> do not significantly interfere with <a href="/wiki/Activities_of_daily_living" title="Activities of daily living">activities of daily living</a> or pose a health-threat to the mother or baby. However, <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_pregnancy" title="Complications of pregnancy">pregnancy complications</a> can cause other more severe symptoms, such as those associated with <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>.
</p><p>Common signs and symptoms of pregnancy include:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medicine)" title="Fatigue (medicine)">Tiredness</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Morning_sickness" title="Morning sickness">Morning sickness</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Constipation" title="Constipation">Constipation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pelvic_girdle_pain" title="Pelvic girdle pain">Pelvic girdle pain</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Back_pain#Pregnancy" title="Back pain">Back pain</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Braxton_Hicks_contractions" title="Braxton Hicks contractions">Braxton Hicks contractions</a>. Occasional, irregular, and often painless contractions that occur several times per day.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peripheral_edema" title="Peripheral edema">Peripheral edema</a> swelling of the lower limbs. Common complaint in advancing pregnancy. Can be caused by <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava_syndrome" title="Inferior vena cava syndrome">inferior vena cava syndrome</a> resulting from compression of the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cava</a> and pelvic veins by the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a> leading to increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_pressure#Medicine" title="Hydrostatic pressure">hydrostatic pressure</a> in lower extremities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">Low blood pressure</a> often caused by compression of both the inferior vena cava and the <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_aorta" title="Abdominal aorta">abdominal aorta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aortocaval_compression_syndrome" title="Aortocaval compression syndrome">aortocaval compression syndrome</a>).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Increased_urinary_frequency" title="Increased urinary frequency">Increased urinary frequency</a>. A common complaint, caused by increased intravascular volume, elevated <a href="/wiki/Glomerular_filtration_rate" title="Glomerular filtration rate">glomerular filtration rate</a>, and compression of the <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">bladder</a> by the expanding uterus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection#Pregnancy" title="Urinary tract infection">Urinary tract infection</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Varicose_veins" title="Varicose veins">Varicose veins</a>. Common complaint caused by relaxation of the venous <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> and increased intravascular pressure.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhoids" title="Hemorrhoids">Hemorrhoids</a> (piles). Swollen <a href="/wiki/Rectal_venous_plexus" title="Rectal venous plexus">veins at or inside the anal area</a>. Caused by impaired venous return, straining associated with constipation, or increased intra-abdominal pressure in later pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE-Vazquez_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-CE-Vazquez-30">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">Regurgitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heartburn" title="Heartburn">heartburn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stretch_marks#Pregnancy" title="Stretch marks">Stretch marks</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breast_tenderness" title="Breast tenderness">Breast tenderness</a> is common during the first trimester, and is more common in women who are pregnant at a young age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Melasma" title="Melasma">Melasma</a>, also known as the mask of pregnancy, is a discoloration, most often of the face. It usually begins to fade several months after giving birth.</li></ul>

<td>36–37 weeks

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Chronology" title="Chronology">chronology</a> of pregnancy is, unless otherwise specified, generally given as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestational_age_(obstetrics)" title="Gestational age (obstetrics)">gestational age</a>, where the starting point is the beginning of the woman's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_menstrual_period" title="Last menstrual period">last menstrual period</a> (LMP), or the corresponding age of the gestation as estimated by a more accurate method if available. This model means that the woman is counted as being "pregnant" two weeks before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conception_(biology)" title="Conception (biology)">conception</a> and three weeks before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Implantation_(human_embryo)" title="Implantation (human embryo)">implantation</a>.  Sometimes, timing may also use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization_age" title="Fertilization age">fertilization age</a>, which is the age of the embryo since conception.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_of_gestational_age">Start of gestational age</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gestational_age" title="Gestational age">Gestational age</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Congress_of_Obstetricians_and_Gynecologists" title="American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists">American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists</a> recommend the following methods to calculate gestational age:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acog2012_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-acog2012-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Directly calculating the days since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">last menstrual period</a>.</li>
<li>Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obstetric_ultrasound" title="Obstetric ultrasound">obstetric ultrasound</a>, comparing the size of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryo" title="Human embryo">embryo</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> to that of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reference_group" title="Reference group">reference group</a> of pregnancies of known gestational age (such as calculated from last menstrual periods), and using the mean gestational age of other embryos or fetuses of the same size. If the gestational age as calculated from an early ultrasound is contradictory to the one calculated directly from the last menstrual period, it is still the one from the early ultrasound that is used for the rest of the pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acog2012_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-acog2012-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>In case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilization" title="In vitro fertilization">in vitro fertilization</a>, calculating days since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oocyte_retrieval" title="Oocyte retrieval">oocyte retrieval</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-incubation" title="Co-incubation">co-incubation</a> and adding 14 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trimesters">Trimesters</span></h3>
<p>Pregnancy is divided into three trimesters, each lasting for approximately three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> The exact length of each trimester can vary between sources. 
<span class="anchor" id="First_trimester"></span><span class="anchor" id="Second_trimester"></span><span class="anchor" id="Third_trimester"></span>
</p>
<ul><li>The <b>first trimester</b> begins with the start of gestational age as described above, that is, the beginning of week 1, or 0 weeks + 0 days of gestational age (GA). It ends at week 12 (11 weeks + 6 days of GA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> or end of week 14 (13 weeks + 6 days of GA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSF_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCSF-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>second trimester</b> is defined as starting, between the beginning of week 13 (12 weeks +0 days of GA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> and beginning of week 15 (14 weeks + 0 days of GA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSF_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-UCSF-36">[36]</a></sup> It ends at the end of week 27 (26 weeks + 6 days of GA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSF_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-UCSF-36">[36]</a></sup> or end of week 28 (27 weeks + 6 days of GA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>third trimester</b> is defined as starting, between the beginning of week 28 (27 weeks + 0 days of GA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSF_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-UCSF-36">[36]</a></sup> or beginning of week 29 (28 weeks + 0 days of GA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-4">[4]</a></sup> It lasts until <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pregnancy_timeline.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="910" data-file-width="5988" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Pregnancy_timeline.png/700px-Pregnancy_timeline.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Pregnancy_timeline.png/1050px-Pregnancy_timeline.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Pregnancy_timeline.png/1400px-Pregnancy_timeline.png 2x" width="700"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pregnancy_timeline.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline of pregnancy, including (from top to bottom): Trimesters, embryo/fetus development, <a href="/wiki/Gestational_age" title="Gestational age">gestational age</a> in weeks and months, viability and maturity stages</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estimation_of_due_date">Estimation of due date</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1079" data-file-width="1305" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg/220px-Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg/330px-Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg/440px-Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Distribution_of_gestational_age_at_childbirth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of <a href="/wiki/Gestational_age" title="Gestational age">gestational age</a> at childbirth among singleton live births, given both when gestational age is estimated by first trimester ultrasound and directly by last menstrual period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoffman2008_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoffman2008-37">[37]</a></sup> Roughly 80% of births occur between 37 and 41 weeks of gestational age.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Estimated_date_of_delivery" title="Estimated date of delivery">Estimated date of delivery</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Estimated_date_of_delivery" title="Estimated date of delivery">Due date estimation</a> basically follows two steps:
</p>
<ul><li>Determination of which time point is to be used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_(number)" title="Origin (number)">origin</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestational_age_(obstetrics)" title="Gestational age (obstetrics)">gestational age</a>, as described in the section above.</li>
<li>Adding the estimated gestational age at childbirth to the above time point. Childbirth on average occurs at a gestational age of 280 days (40 weeks), which is therefore often used as a standard estimation for individual pregnancies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Healthline_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Healthline-38">[38]</a></sup> However, alternative durations as well as more individualized methods have also been suggested.</li></ul>
<p><i>Naegele's rule</i> is a standard way of calculating the due date for a pregnancy when assuming a gestational age of 280 days at childbirth. The rule estimates the expected date of delivery (EDD) by adding a year, subtracting three months, and adding seven days to the origin of gestational age. Alternatively there are <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">mobile apps</a>, which essentially always give consistent estimations compared to each other and correct for <a href="/wiki/Leap_year" title="Leap year">leap year</a>, while pregnancy wheels made of paper can differ from each other by 7 days and generally do not correct for leap year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24036402_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24036402-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, actual childbirth has only a certain probability of occurring within the limits of the estimated due date. A study of singleton live births came to the result that childbirth has a <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviation</a> of 14 days when gestational age is estimated by first trimester <a href="/wiki/Obstetric_ultrasonography" title="Obstetric ultrasonography">ultrasound</a>, and 16 days when estimated directly by last menstrual period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoffman2008_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-hoffman2008-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacity">Capacity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">Female reproductive system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy" title="Teenage pregnancy">Teenage pregnancy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_over_age_50" title="Pregnancy over age 50">Pregnancy over age 50</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transgender_pregnancy" title="Transgender pregnancy">Transgender pregnancy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Male_pregnancy" title="Male pregnancy">Male pregnancy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">Fertility</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fecundity" title="Fecundity">fecundity</a> are the respective capacities to <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">fertilize</a> and establish a clinical pregnancy and have a life birth. <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">Infertility</a> is the impairment to establish a clinical pregnancy and <a href="/wiki/Sterility_(physiology)" title="Sterility (physiology)">sterility</a> is the permanent impairment to establish a clinical pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zegers-Hochschild_Adamson_Dyer_Racowsky_2017_pp._393–406_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zegers-Hochschild_Adamson_Dyer_Racowsky_2017_pp._393–406-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The capacity for pregnancy depends on the <a href="/wiki/Human_reproductive_system" title="Human reproductive system">reproductive system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_reproductive_system" title="Development of the reproductive system">its development</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variations_in_sex_characteristics" title="Variations in sex characteristics">its variation</a>, as well as on the condition of a person. 
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women" title="Women">Women</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a> people who have a functioning <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">female reproductive system</a> are capable of pregnancy. In some cases, someone might be able to produce fertilizable eggs, but might not have a womb or none that can sufficiently gestate, in which case they might find <a href="/wiki/Surrogacy" title="Surrogacy">surrogacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhs.uk_2021_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhs.uk_2021-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiation">Initiation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">Human fertilization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_Fertilization.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Human_Fertilization.png/220px-Human_Fertilization.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Human_Fertilization.png/330px-Human_Fertilization.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Human_Fertilization.png/440px-Human_Fertilization.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_Fertilization.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">Fertilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Implantation_(embryology)" title="Implantation (embryology)">implantation</a> in humans</div></div></div>
<p>Through an interplay of hormones that includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Follicle_stimulating_hormone" title="Follicle stimulating hormone">follicle stimulating hormone</a> that stimulates <a href="/wiki/Folliculogenesis" title="Folliculogenesis">folliculogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oogenesis" title="Oogenesis">oogenesis</a> creates a mature <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">egg cell</a>, the female <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gamete</a>. <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">Fertilization</a> is the event where the egg cell fuses with the male gamete, <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoon</a>. After the point of fertilization, the fused product of the female and male gamete is referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a> or fertilized egg. The fusion of female and male gametes usually occurs following the act of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_rate#Pregnancy_rate_for_sexual_intercourse" title="Pregnancy rate">Pregnancy rates for sexual intercourse</a> are highest during the <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">menstrual cycle</a> time from some 5 days before until 1 to 2 days after ovulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Fertilization can also occur by <a href="/wiki/Assisted_reproductive_technology" title="Assisted reproductive technology">assisted reproductive technology</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">artificial insemination</a> and <a href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilisation" title="In vitro fertilisation">in vitro fertilisation</a>.
</p><p>Fertilization (conception) is sometimes used as the initiation of pregnancy, with the derived age being termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization_age" title="Fertilization age">fertilization age</a>. Fertilization usually occurs about two weeks before the <i>next</i> expected menstrual period.
</p><p>A third point in time is also considered by some people to be the true beginning of a pregnancy: This is time of implantation, when the future fetus attaches to the lining of the uterus. This is about a week to ten days after fertilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berger-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_embryo_and_fetus">Development of embryo and fetus</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_embryonic_development" title="Human embryonic development">Human embryonic development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">Prenatal development</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">Fetus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HumanEmbryogenesis.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1220" data-file-width="1560" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/HumanEmbryogenesis.svg/220px-HumanEmbryogenesis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/HumanEmbryogenesis.svg/330px-HumanEmbryogenesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/HumanEmbryogenesis.svg/440px-HumanEmbryogenesis.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HumanEmbryogenesis.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The initial stages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">human embryogenesis</a></div></div></div>
<p>The sperm and the egg cell, which has been released from one of the female's two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovaries" title="Ovaries">ovaries</a>, unite in one of the two <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube" title="Fallopian tube">Fallopian tubes</a>. The fertilized egg, known as a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a>, then moves toward the uterus, a journey that can take up to a week to complete. Cell division begins approximately 24 to 36 hours after the female and male cells unite. Cell division continues at a rapid rate and the cells then develop into what is known as a <a href="/wiki/Blastocyst" title="Blastocyst">blastocyst</a>. The blastocyst arrives at the uterus and attaches to the uterine wall, a process known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Implantation_(human_embryo)" title="Implantation (human embryo)">implantation</a>.
</p><p>The development of the mass of cells that will become the infant is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">embryogenesis</a> during the first approximately ten weeks of gestation. During this time, cells begin to differentiate into the various body systems. The basic outlines of the organ, body, and nervous systems are established. By the end of the embryonic stage, the beginnings of features such as fingers, eyes, mouth, and ears become visible. Also during this time, there is development of structures important to the support of the embryo, including the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umbilical_cord" title="Umbilical cord">umbilical cord</a>. The placenta connects the developing embryo to the uterine wall to allow nutrient uptake, waste elimination, and gas exchange via the mother's blood supply. The umbilical cord is the connecting cord from the embryo or fetus to the placenta.
</p><p>After about ten weeks of gestational age—which is the same as eight weeks after conception—the embryo becomes known as a <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSD_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSD-44">[44]</a></sup> At the beginning of the fetal stage, the risk of miscarriage decreases sharply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sharply_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-sharply-45">[45]</a></sup> At this stage, a fetus is about 30 mm (1.2 inches) in length, the heartbeat is seen via ultrasound, and the fetus makes involuntary motions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prechtl_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prechtl-46">[46]</a></sup> During continued fetal development, the early body systems, and structures that were established in the embryonic stage continue to develop. Sex organs begin to appear during the third month of gestation. The fetus continues to grow in both weight and length, although the majority of the physical growth occurs in the last weeks of pregnancy.
</p><p>Electrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_activity" title="Brain activity">brain activity</a> is first detected at the end of week 5 of gestation, but as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain-death" title="Brain-death">brain-dead</a> patients, it is primitive neural activity rather than the beginning of conscious brain activity. Synapses do not begin to form until week 17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Neural connections between the <a href="/wiki/Sensory_cortex" title="Sensory cortex">sensory cortex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">thalamus</a> develop as early as 24 weeks' gestational age, but the first evidence of their function does not occur until around 30 weeks, when minimal <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dreaming</a>, and the ability to feel pain emerges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the fetus begins to move during the first trimester, it is not until the second trimester that movement, known as <a href="/wiki/Quickening" title="Quickening">quickening</a>, can be felt. This typically happens in the fourth month, more specifically in the 20th to 21st week, or by the 19th week if the woman has been pregnant before. It is common for some women not to feel the fetus move until much later. During the second trimester, when the body size changes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternity_clothes" title="Maternity clothes">maternity clothes</a> may be worn.
</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
